<p><b>Alsace</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'">eɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'">eɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <small>French: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[al.zas]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<a href="/wiki/File:Fr-Paris--Alsace.ogg" title="File:Fr-Paris--Alsace.ogg"><img alt="" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a> <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Fr-Alsace.ogg" title="Fr-Alsace.ogg">listen</a>)</small>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alsatian_language" title="Alsatian language">Alsatian</a>: <i>’s Elsass</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Alemannic_German" title="Help:IPA for Alemannic German">[ˈɛlsɑs]</a></span>; German: <i>Elsass</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_German" title="Help:IPA for German">[ˈɛlzas]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<a href="/wiki/File:Elsass.ogg" title="File:Elsass.ogg"><img alt="" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a> <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Elsass.ogg" title="Elsass.ogg">listen</a>)</small>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Alsatia</i></span>) is a cultural and historical region in eastern <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, now located in the administrative region of <a href="/wiki/Grand_Est" title="Grand Est">Grand Est</a>. Alsace is located on France's eastern border and on the west bank of the upper <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>.</p>
<p>From 1982 until January 2016, Alsace was the smallest of 22 <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_France" title="Regions of France">administrative regions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_France" title="Metropolitan France">metropolitan France</a>, consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Bas-Rhin" title="Bas-Rhin">Bas-Rhin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haut-Rhin" title="Haut-Rhin">Haut-Rhin</a> <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_France" title="Departments of France">departments</a>. <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_France#Reform_and_mergers_of_regions" title="Regions of France">Territorial reform</a> passed by the French legislature in 2014 resulted in the merger of the Alsace administrative region with <a href="/wiki/Champagne-Ardenne" title="Champagne-Ardenne">Champagne-Ardenne</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorraine_(region)" title="Lorraine (region)">Lorraine</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Grand_Est" title="Grand Est">Grand Est</a>.</p>
<p>The predominant historical language of Alsace is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alsatian_language" title="Alsatian language">Alsatian</a>, a Germanic (mainly <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic</a>) dialect also spoken across the Rhine, but today most Alsatians primarily speak French, the official language of France. The political status of Alsace has been heavily influenced by historical decisions, wars, and strategic politics. The economic and cultural capital as well as largest city of Alsace is <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>. The city is the seat of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Institutions_in_Strasbourg" title="European Institutions in Strasbourg">several international organizations and bodies</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name "Alsace" can be traced to the <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>Ali-saz</i> or <i>Elisaz</i>, meaning "foreign domain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> An alternative explanation is from a <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> <i>Ell-sass</i>, meaning "seated on the <a href="/wiki/Ill_(France)" title="Ill (France)">Ill</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> a river in Alsace.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Alsace-Lorraine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The region, as part of Lorraine, was part of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, and then was gradually annexed by France in the 17th century, and formalized as one of the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_France" title="Provinces of France">provinces of France</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinist" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> manufacturing republic of <a href="/wiki/Mulhouse" title="Mulhouse">Mulhouse</a>, known as <i>Stadtrepublik Mülhausen</i>, joined France and became a part of Alsace after a vote by its citizens on 4 January 1798. Alsace is frequently mentioned with and as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorraine_(region)" title="Lorraine (region)">Lorraine</a> and the former duchy of Lorraine, since it was a vital part of the duchy, and later because German possession as the imperial province (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reichsland_Elsa%C3%9F-Lothringen" title="Reichsland Elsaß-Lothringen">Alsace-Lorraine</a>, 1871–1918) was contested in the 19th and 20th centuries; France and Germany exchanged control of parts of Lorraine (including Alsace) four times in 75 years.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In prehistoric times, Alsace was inhabited by nomadic hunters.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Roman_Alsace">Pre-Roman Alsace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pre-Roman Alsace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1500 BC,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celt" title="Celt">Celts</a> began to settle in Alsace, clearing and cultivating the land. It should be noted that Alsace is a plain surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Vosges" title="Vosges">Vosges</a> mountains (west) and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Forest" title="Black Forest">Black Forest</a> mountains (east). It creates <a href="/wiki/Foehn_wind" title="Foehn wind">Foehn winds</a> which, along with natural irrigation, contributes to the fertility of the soil. In a world of agriculture, Alsace has always been a rich region which explains why it suffered so many invasions and annexations in its history.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Alsace">Roman Alsace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Roman Alsace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 58 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> had invaded and established Alsace as a center of <a href="/wiki/Viticulture" title="Viticulture">viticulture</a>. To protect this highly valued industry, the Romans built fortifications and military camps that evolved into various communities which have been inhabited continuously to the present day. While part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, Alsace was part of <a href="/wiki/Germania_Superior" title="Germania Superior">Germania Superior</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alemannic_and_Frankish_Alsace">Alemannic and Frankish Alsace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Alemannic and Frankish Alsace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Alsace" title="Duchy of Alsace">Duchy of Alsace</a></div>
<p>With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">decline of the Roman Empire</a>, Alsace became the territory of the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a>. The Alemanni were agricultural people, and their Germanic language formed the basis of modern-day dialects spoken along the Upper Rhine (Alsatian, Alemannian, Swabian, Swiss). <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> defeated the Alemanni during the 5th century AD, culminating with the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tolbiac" title="Battle of Tolbiac">Battle of Tolbiac</a>, and Alsace became part of the <a href="/wiki/Austrasia" title="Austrasia">Kingdom of Austrasia</a>. Under Clovis' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merovingian" title="Merovingian">Merovingian</a> successors the inhabitants were Christianized. Alsace remained under Frankish control until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_realm" title="Frankish realm">Frankish realm</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">Oaths of Strasbourg</a> of 842, was formally dissolved in 843 at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Verdun" title="Treaty of Verdun">Treaty of Verdun</a>; the grandsons of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> divided the realm into three parts. Alsace formed part of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Francia" title="Middle Francia">Middle Francia</a>, which was ruled by the eldest grandson <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lothar_I" title="Lothar I">Lothar I</a>. Lothar died early in 855 and his realm was divided into three parts. The part known as <a href="/wiki/Lotharingia" title="Lotharingia">Lotharingia</a>, or Lorraine, was given to Lothar's son. The rest was shared between Lothar's brothers <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">Charles the Bald</a> (ruler of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Francia" title="Western Francia">West Frankish</a> realm) and <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_German" title="Louis the German">Louis the German</a> (ruler of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Francia" title="Eastern Francia">East Frankish</a> realm). The Kingdom of Lotharingia was short-lived, however, becoming the <a href="/wiki/Stem_duchy" title="Stem duchy">stem duchy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Lorraine" title="Duchy of Lorraine">Lorraine</a> in Eastern Francia after the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ribemont" title="Treaty of Ribemont">Treaty of Ribemont</a> in 880. Alsace was united with the other Alemanni east of the Rhine into the stem duchy of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Swabia" title="Duchy of Swabia">Swabia</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alsace_within_the_Holy_Roman_Empire">Alsace within the Holy Roman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Alsace within the Holy Roman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At about this time the surrounding areas experienced recurring fragmentation and reincorporations among a number of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> secular and ecclesiastical lordships, a common process in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. Alsace experienced great prosperity during the 12th and 13th centuries under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" title="House of Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufen emperors</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I</a> set up Alsace as a province (a <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Procuratio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Procuratio (page does not exist)">procuratio</a></i>, not a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Provincia" title="Provincia">provincia</a></i>) to be ruled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministeriales" title="Ministeriales">ministeriales</a>, a non-noble class of civil servants. The idea was that such men would be more tractable and less likely to alienate the <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fief</a> from the crown out of their own greed. The province had a single provincial court (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landgericht" title="Landgericht">Landgericht</a>) and a central administration with its seat at <a href="/wiki/Haguenau" title="Haguenau">Hagenau</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick II</a> designated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Strasbourg" title="Bishop of Strasbourg">Bishop of Strasbourg</a> to administer Alsace, but the authority of the bishop was challenged by Count <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rudolf_of_Habsburg" title="Rudolf of Habsburg">Rudolf of Habsburg</a>, who received his rights from Frederick II's son <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad IV</a>. Strasbourg began to grow to become the most populous and commercially important town in the region. In 1262, after a long struggle with the ruling bishops, its citizens gained the status of <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">free imperial city</a>. A stop on the Paris-<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>-<a href="/wiki/Orient" title="Orient">Orient</a> trade route, as well as a port on the Rhine route linking <a href="/wiki/Southern_Germany" title="Southern Germany">southern Germany</a> and Switzerland to the Netherlands, England and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, it became the political and economic center of the region. Cities such as <a href="/wiki/Colmar" title="Colmar">Colmar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hagenau" title="Hagenau">Hagenau</a> also began to grow in economic importance and gained a kind of autonomy within the "Decapole" or "Dekapolis", a federation of ten free towns.</p>
<p>As in much of Europe, the prosperity of Alsace came to an end in the 14th century by a series of harsh winters, bad harvests, and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>. These hardships were blamed on Jews, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">pogroms</a> of 1336 and 1339. In 1349, Jews of Alsace were accused of poisoning the wells with <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a>, leading to the massacre of thousands of Jews during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strasbourg_pogrom" title="Strasbourg pogrom">Strasbourg pogrom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Jews were subsequently forbidden to settle in the town. An additional natural disaster was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhine_rift" title="Rhine rift">Rhine rift</a> earthquake of 1356, one of Europe's worst which made ruins of <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>. Prosperity returned to Alsace under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habsburg" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a> administration during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg/220px-Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg/330px-Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg/440px-Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Absolute_Petite_France_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Petite_France,_Strasbourg" title="Petite France, Strasbourg">Petite France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Holy Roman Empire central power had begun to decline following years of imperial adventures in Italian lands, often ceding hegemony in Western Europe to France, which had long since centralized power. France began an aggressive policy of expanding eastward, first to the rivers <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meuse" title="Meuse">Meuse</a>, and when those borders were reached, aiming for the Rhine. In 1299, the French proposed a marriage alliance between <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a>'s sister <a href="/wiki/Blanche_of_France,_Duchess_of_Austria" title="Blanche of France, Duchess of Austria">Blanche</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_I_of_Germany" title="Albert I of Germany">Albert I of Germany</a>'s son <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_I_of_Bohemia" title="Rudolf I of Bohemia">Rudolf</a>, with Alsace to be the dowry; however, the deal never came off. In 1307, the town of <a href="/wiki/Belfort" title="Belfort">Belfort</a> was first chartered by the Counts of <a href="/wiki/Montb%C3%A9liard" title="Montbéliard">Montbéliard</a>. During the next century, France was to be militarily shattered by the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>, which prevented for a time any further tendencies in this direction. After the conclusion of the war, France was again free to pursue its desire to reach the Rhine and in 1444 a French army appeared in Lorraine and Alsace. It took up winter quarters, demanded the submission of <a href="/wiki/Metz" title="Metz">Metz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a> and launched an attack on <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>.</p>
<p>In 1469, following the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Treaty_of_St._Omer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Treaty of St. Omer (page does not exist)">Treaty of St. Omer</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trait%C3%A9_de_Saint-Omer" title="fr:Traité de Saint-Omer">fr</a>)</span>, Upper Alsace was sold by Archduke <a href="/wiki/Sigismund,_Archduke_of_Austria" title="Sigismund, Archduke of Austria">Sigismund of Austria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bold" title="Charles the Bold">Charles the Bold</a>, Duke of Burgundy. Although Charles was the nominal landlord, taxes were paid to <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick III, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick III, Holy Roman Emperor</a>. The latter was able to use this tax and a dynastic marriage to his advantage to gain back full control of Upper Alsace (apart from the free towns, but including Belfort) in 1477 when it became part of the demesne of the Habsburg family, who were also rulers of the empire. The town of Mulhouse joined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_Confederation" title="Swiss Confederation">Swiss Confederation</a> in 1515, where it was to remain until 1798.</p>
<p>By the time of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> in the 16th century, Strasbourg was a prosperous community, and its inhabitants accepted Protestantism in 1523. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bucer" title="Martin Bucer">Martin Bucer</a> was a prominent Protestant reformer in the region. His efforts were countered by the Roman Catholic Habsburgs who tried to eradicate heresy in Upper Alsace. As a result, Alsace was transformed into a mosaic of Catholic and Protestant territories. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Montb%C3%A9liard" title="Montbéliard">Mömpelgard (Montbéliard)</a> to the southwest of Alsace, belonging to the Counts of <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Württemberg">Württemberg</a> since 1397, remained a Protestant enclave in France until 1793.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incorporation_into_France">Incorporation into France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Incorporation into France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This situation prevailed until 1639, when most of Alsace was conquered by France so as to keep it out of the hands of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Habsburgs" title="Spanish Habsburgs">Spanish Habsburgs</a>, who by <a href="/wiki/O%C3%B1ate_treaty" title="Oñate treaty">secret treaty</a> in 1617 had gained a clear road to their valuable and rebellious possessions in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Netherlands" title="Spanish Netherlands">Spanish Netherlands</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Road" title="Spanish Road">Spanish Road</a>. Beset by enemies and seeking to gain a free hand in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary_(1526%E2%80%931867)" title="Kingdom of Hungary (1526–1867)">Hungary</a>, the Habsburgs sold their <a href="/wiki/Sundgau" title="Sundgau">Sundgau</a> territory (mostly in Upper Alsace) to France in 1646, which had occupied it, for the sum of 1.2 million <a href="/wiki/Thaler" title="Thaler">Thalers</a>. When hostilities were concluded in 1648 with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Westphalia" title="Treaty of Westphalia">Treaty of Westphalia</a>, most of Alsace was recognized as part of France, although some towns remained independent. The treaty stipulations regarding Alsace were complex; although the French king gained sovereignty, existing rights and customs of the inhabitants were largely preserved. France continued to maintain its customs border along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vosges_mountains" title="Vosges mountains">Vosges mountains</a> where it had been, leaving Alsace more economically oriented to neighbouring German-speaking lands. The German language remained in use in local administration, in schools, and at the (Lutheran) <a href="/wiki/University_of_Strasbourg" title="University of Strasbourg">University of Strasbourg</a>, which continued to draw students from other German-speaking lands. The 1685 <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Fontainebleau" title="Edict of Fontainebleau">Edict of Fontainebleau</a>, by which the French king ordered the suppression of <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">French Protestantism</a>, was not applied in Alsace. France did endeavour to promote Catholicism; <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_Cathedral" title="Strasbourg Cathedral">Strasbourg Cathedral</a>, for example, which had been Lutheran from 1524 to 1681, was returned to the Catholic Church. However, compared to the rest of France, Alsace enjoyed a climate of religious tolerance.</p>
<p>The warfare that had partially depopulated the region created opportunities for a stream of immigrants from Switzerland, Germany, Austria, Lorraine, <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoy</a> and other lands that continued until the mid-18th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2414" data-file-width="3009" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg/220px-Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg/330px-Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg/440px-Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_XIV_receiving_the_keys_of_Strasbourg-Constantyn_Francken-f3791425.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> receiving the keys of Strasbourg in 1681</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>France consolidated its hold with the 1679 <a href="/wiki/Treaties_of_Nijmegen" title="Treaties of Nijmegen">Treaties of Nijmegen</a>, which brought most remaining towns under its control. France seized Strasbourg in 1681 in an unprovoked action. These territorial changes were recognised in the 1697 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ryswick" title="Treaty of Ryswick">Treaty of Ryswick</a> that ended the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_of_the_Grand_Alliance" title="War of the Grand Alliance">War of the Grand Alliance</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Revolution">French Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: French Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3523" data-file-width="2742" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg/220px-Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg/330px-Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg/440px-Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enseigne_Alsacienne_revolutionnaire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alsatian sign, 1792:<br/>
<i>Freiheit Gleichheit Brüderlichk. od. Tod</i> (Liberty Equality Fraternity or Death)<br/>
<i>Tod den Tyranen</i> (Death to Tyrants)<br/>
<i>Heil den Völkern</i> (Long live the Peoples)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The year 1789 brought the French Revolution and with it the first division of Alsace into the départements of <a href="/wiki/Haut-Rhin" title="Haut-Rhin">Haut-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bas-Rhin" title="Bas-Rhin">Bas-Rhin</a>. Alsatians played an active role in the French Revolution. On 21 July 1789, after receiving news of the <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">Storming of the Bastille</a> in Paris, a crowd of people stormed the Strasbourg city hall, forcing the city administrators to flee and putting symbolically an end to the feudal system in Alsace. In 1792, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Joseph_Rouget_de_Lisle" title="Claude Joseph Rouget de Lisle">Rouget de Lisle</a> composed in Strasbourg the Revolutionary marching song "<a href="/wiki/La_Marseillaise" title="La Marseillaise">La Marseillaise</a>" (as <i>Marching song for the Army of the Rhine</i>), which later became the anthem of France. "La Marseillaise" was played for the first time in April of that year in front of the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_(France)" title="Mayor (France)">mayor</a> of Strasbourg <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippe-Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_de_Dietrich" title="Philippe-Frédéric de Dietrich">Philippe-Frédéric de Dietrich</a>. Some of the most famous generals of the French Revolution also came from Alsace, notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Christophe_Kellermann" title="François Christophe Kellermann">Kellermann</a>, the victor of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Valmy" title="Battle of Valmy">Valmy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean_Baptiste_Kl%C3%A9ber" title="Jean Baptiste Kléber">Kléber</a>, who led the armies of the French Republic in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolt_in_the_Vend%C3%A9e" title="Revolt in the Vendée">Vendée</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Joseph_Westermann" title="François Joseph Westermann">Westermann</a>, who also fought in the Vendée.</p>
<p>At the same time, some Alsatians were in opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Jacobin_(politics)" title="Jacobin (politics)">Jacobins</a> and sympathetic to the invading forces of <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a> who sought to crush the nascent <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_republic" title="Revolutionary republic">revolutionary republic</a>. Many of the residents of the <a href="/wiki/Sundgau" title="Sundgau">Sundgau</a> made "pilgrimages" to places like <a href="/wiki/Mariastein_Abbey" title="Mariastein Abbey">Mariastein Abbey</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>, in Switzerland, for baptisms and weddings. When the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Army" title="French Revolutionary Army">French Revolutionary Army</a> of the Rhine was victorious, tens of thousands fled east before it. When they were later permitted to return (in some cases not until 1799), it was often to find that their lands and homes had been confiscated. These conditions led to emigration by hundreds of families to newly vacant lands in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> in 1803–4 and again in 1808. A poignant retelling of this event based on what <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a> had personally witnessed can be found in his long poem <i><a href="/wiki/Hermann_and_Dorothea" title="Hermann and Dorothea">Hermann and Dorothea</a></i>.</p>
<p>In response to the restoration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon I of France</a> in 1815, Alsace along with other frontier provinces of France was occupied by foreign forces from 1815 to 1818,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> including over 280,000 soldiers and 90,000 horses in Bas-Rhin alone. This had grave effects on trade and the economy of the region since former overland trade routes were switched to newly opened <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> seaports.</p>
<p>The population grew rapidly, from 800,000 in 1814 to 914,000 in 1830 and 1,067,000 in 1846. The combination of economic and demographic factors led to hunger, housing shortages and a lack of work for young people. Thus, it is not surprising that people left Alsace, not only for Paris – where the Alsatian community grew in numbers, with famous members such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baron_Haussmann" title="Baron Haussmann">Baron Haussmann</a> – but also for more distant places like Russia and the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a>, to take advantage of the new opportunities offered there: Austria had conquered lands in Eastern Europe from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and offered generous terms to colonists as a way of consolidating its hold on the new territories. Many Alsatians also began to sail to the United States, settling in many areas from 1820 to 1850.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1843 and 1844, sailing ships bringing immigrant families from Alsace arrived at the port of New York. Some settled in Illinois, many to farm or to seek success in commercial ventures: for example, the sailing ships <i>Sully</i> (in May 1843) and <i>Iowa</i> (in June 1844) brought families who set up homes in northern Illinois and northern Indiana. Some Alsatian immigrants were noted for their roles in 19th-century American economic development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Others ventured to Canada to settle in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterloo_County" title="Waterloo County">Waterloo County</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jews">Jews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Jews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Alsace" title="History of the Jews in Alsace">History of the Jews in Alsace</a></div>
<p>By 1790, the Jewish population of Alsace was approximately 22,500, about 3% of the provincial population. They were highly segregated and subject to long-standing anti-Jewish regulations. They maintained their own customs, Yiddish language, and historic traditions within the tightly-knit ghettos; they adhered to Talmudic law enforced by their rabbis. Jews were barred from most cities and instead lived in villages. They concentrated in trade, services, and especially in money lending. They financed about a third of the mortgages in Alsace. Official tolerance grew during the French Revolution, with full emancipation in 1791. However, local antisemitism also increased and Napoleon turned hostile in 1806, imposing a one-year moratorium on all debts owed to Jews.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the 1830-1870 era most Jews moved to the cities, where they integrated and acculturated, as antisemitism sharply declined. By 1831, the state began paying salaries to official rabbis, and in 1846 a special legal oath for Jews was discontinued. Antisemitic local riots occasionally occurred, especially during the Revolution of 1848. The merger of Alsace into Germany in 1871-1918 lessened antisemitic violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Between_France_and_Germany">Between France and Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Between France and Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a></div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><i>We Germans who know Germany and France know better what is good for the Alsatians than the unfortunates themselves. In the perversion of their French life they have no exact idea of what concerns Germany.</i></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Treitschke" title="Heinrich von Treitschke">Heinrich von Treitschke</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_nationalist" title="German nationalist">German nationalist</a> historian and politician, 1871<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alsace4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1428" data-file-width="1905" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alsace4.jpg/220px-Alsace4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alsace4.jpg/330px-Alsace4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alsace4.jpg/440px-Alsace4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alsace4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Traditional costumes of Alsace</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_Franco-Prussian_War" title="Causes of the Franco-Prussian War">started</a> in July 1870, saw France defeated in May 1871 by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a> and other German states. The end of the war led to the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">unification of Germany</a>. <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> annexed Alsace and northern Lorraine to the new <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> in 1871; France ceded more than nine-tenths of Alsace and one-fourth of Lorraine as stipulated in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Frankfurt_(1871)" title="Treaty of Frankfurt (1871)">treaty of Frankfurt</a>, <i>de jure</i>, that wasn't an annexation any more.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Unlike other members states of the German federation, which had governments of their own, the new <i>Imperial territory of Alsace-Lorraine</i> was under the sole authority of the <a href="/wiki/Kaiser" title="Kaiser">Kaiser</a>, administered directly by the imperial government in Berlin. Between 100,000 and 130,000 Alsatians (of a total population of about a million and a half) chose to remain French citizens and leave <i>Reichsland Elsaß-Lothringen</i>, many of them resettling in <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">French Algeria</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pieds-Noirs" title="Pieds-Noirs">Pieds-Noirs</a>. Only in 1911 was Alsace-Lorraine granted some measure of autonomy, which was manifested also in a flag and an anthem (<a href="/wiki/Els%C3%A4ssisches_Fahnenlied" title="Elsässisches Fahnenlied">Elsässisches Fahnenlied</a>). In 1913, however, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saverne_Affair" title="Saverne Affair">Saverne Affair</a> (French: Incident de Saverne) showed the limits of this new tolerance of the Alsatian identity.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="694" data-file-width="500" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg/170px-Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg/255px-Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg/340px-Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adolphe_Braun_Alsace_costume.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An Alsatian woman in traditional costume, photographed by <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_Braun" title="Adolphe Braun">Adolphe Braun</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the First World War, to avoid ground fights between brothers, many Alsatians served as sailors in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaiserliche_Marine" title="Kaiserliche Marine">Kaiserliche Marine</a> and took part in the Naval mutinies that led to the abdication of the Kaiser in November 1918, which left Alsace-Lorraine without a nominal head of state. The sailors returned home and tried to found a republic. While <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Peirotes" title="Jacques Peirotes">Jacques Peirotes</a>, at this time deputy at the <i>Landrat Elsass-Lothringen</i> and just elected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_mayors_of_Strasbourg" title="List of mayors of Strasbourg">mayor of Strasbourg</a>, proclaimed the forfeiture of the German Empire and the advent of the French Republic, a self-proclaimed government of Alsace-Lorraine declared independence as the "<a href="/wiki/November_1918_in_Alsace-Lorraine" title="November 1918 in Alsace-Lorraine">Republic of Alsace-Lorraine</a>". French troops entered Alsace less than two weeks later to quash the worker strikes and remove the newly established Soviets and revolutionaries from power. At the arrival of the French soldiers, many Alsatians and local Prussian/German administrators and bureaucrats cheered the re-establishment of order<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Although U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> had insisted that the <i>région</i> was self-ruling by legal status, as its constitution had stated it was bound to the sole authority of the Kaiser and not to the German state, France tolerated no plebiscite, as granted by the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> to some eastern German territories at this time, because Alsatians were considered by the French public as fellow Frenchmen liberated from German rule. Germany ceded the region to France under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>.</p>
<p>Policies forbidding the use of German and requiring that of French were introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> However, in order not to antagonize the Alsatians, the region was not subjected to some legal changes that had occurred in the rest of France between 1871 and 1919, such as the 1905 French Law of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1905_French_law_on_the_separation_of_Church_and_State" title="1905 French law on the separation of Church and State">Separation of Church and State</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2039" data-file-width="2235" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG/170px-Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG/255px-Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG/340px-Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ensemble_Timbres_Hindenburg_surcharg%C3%A9s_par_Elsass.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German stamps of <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Hindenburg</a> marked with "Elsaß" (1940)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alsace-Lorraine was occupied by Germany in 1940 during the Second World War. Although Germany never formally annexed Alsace-Lorraine, it was incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Greater German Reich</a>, which had been restructured into <a href="/wiki/Reichsgau" title="Reichsgau">Reichsgaue</a>. Alsace was merged with <a href="/wiki/Baden" title="Baden">Baden</a>, and Lorraine with the <a href="/wiki/Saarland" title="Saarland">Saarland</a>, to become part of a planned <a href="/wiki/Gau_Westmark" title="Gau Westmark">Westmark</a>. During the war, 130,000 young men from Alsace and Lorraine were inducted into the German army against their will (<a href="/wiki/Malgr%C3%A9-nous" title="Malgré-nous">malgré-nous</a>) and in some cases, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waffen_SS" title="Waffen SS">Waffen SS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Some of the latter were involved in war crimes such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oradour-sur-Glane" title="Oradour-sur-Glane">Oradour-sur-Glane</a> massacre. Most of them perished on the eastern front. The few that could escape fled to Switzerland or joined the resistance. In July 1944, 1500 <a href="/wiki/Malgr%C3%A9-nous" title="Malgré-nous">malgré-nous</a> were released from Soviet captivity and sent to <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a>, where they joined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_French_Forces" title="Free French Forces">Free French Forces</a>.</p>
<p>Today the territory is in certain areas subject to some laws that are significantly different from the rest of France – this is known as the <a href="/wiki/Local_law_in_Alsace-Moselle" title="Local law in Alsace-Moselle">local law</a>.</p>
<p>In more recent years, Alsatian is again being promoted by local, national and European authorities as an element of the region's identity. Alsatian is taught in schools (but not mandatory) as one of the regional languages of France. German is also taught as a foreign language in local <a href="/wiki/Kindergarten" title="Kindergarten">kindergartens</a> and schools. However, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_France" title="Constitution of France">Constitution of France</a> still requires that French be the only official language of the Republic.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alsace has a semi-<a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental climate</a> with cold and dry winters and hot summers. There is little precipitation because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vosges_mountains" title="Vosges mountains">Vosges</a> protect it from the west. The city of <a href="/wiki/Colmar" title="Colmar">Colmar</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunny</a> <a href="/wiki/Microclimate" title="Microclimate">microclimate</a>; it is the second driest city in France, with an annual precipitation of just 550 mm, making it ideal for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vin_d%27Alsace" title="Vin d'Alsace">vin d'Alsace</a></i> (<i>Alsatian wine</i>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Topography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="644" data-file-width="443" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg/220px-Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg/330px-Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg/440px-Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alsaceregionsnaturelles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Topographic map of Alsace</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alsace has an area of 8,283 km<sup>2</sup>, making it the smallest <i><a href="/wiki/Regions_of_France" title="Regions of France">région</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_France" title="Metropolitan France">metropolitan France</a>. It is almost four times longer than it is wide, corresponding to a plain between the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> in the east and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vosges_mountains" title="Vosges mountains">Vosges mountains</a> in the west.</p>
<p>It includes the <i><a href="/wiki/Departments_of_France" title="Departments of France">départements</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Haut-Rhin" title="Haut-Rhin">Haut-Rhin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bas-Rhin" title="Bas-Rhin">Bas-Rhin</a> (known previously as <a href="/wiki/Sundgau" title="Sundgau">Sundgau</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nordgau_(Alsace)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nordgau (Alsace) (page does not exist)">Nordgau</a>). It borders Germany on the north and the east, Switzerland and <a href="/wiki/Franche-Comt%C3%A9" title="Franche-Comté">Franche-Comté</a> on the south, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorraine_(region)" title="Lorraine (region)">Lorraine</a> on the west.</p>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a> are also found in the <i>région</i>. Its highest point is the <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Ballon" title="Grand Ballon">Grand Ballon</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Haut-Rhin" title="Haut-Rhin">Haut-Rhin</a>, which reaches a height of 1426 m.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ried_(natural_region)" title="Ried (natural region)">ried</a> lies along the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grandballonsud.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="600" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Grandballonsud.jpg/220px-Grandballonsud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Grandballonsud.jpg/330px-Grandballonsud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Grandballonsud.jpg/440px-Grandballonsud.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grandballonsud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Ballon" title="Grand Ballon">Grand Ballon</a>, southern face, seen from the valley of the <a href="/wiki/Thur_(France)" title="Thur (France)">Thur</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alsace is the part of the plain of the Rhine located at the west of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>, on its left bank. It is a <a href="/wiki/Rift" title="Rift">rift</a> or <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">graben</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">epoch</a>, associated with its <a href="/wiki/Horst_(geology)" title="Horst (geology)">horsts</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vosges_Mountains" title="Vosges Mountains">Vosges</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Forest" title="Black Forest">Black Forest</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Jura_Mountains" title="Jura Mountains">Jura Mountains</a>, formed by slip (induced by the alpine uplift) of the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> cover on the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> formations, goes through the area of <a href="/wiki/Belfort" title="Belfort">Belfort</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vosges_and_Jura_coal_mining_basins" title="Vosges and Jura coal mining basins">Vosges and Jura coal mining basins</a></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It contains many forests, primarily in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vosges_Mountains" title="Vosges Mountains">Vosges</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Bas-Rhin" title="Bas-Rhin">Bas-Rhin</a> (Haguenau Forest).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alsace_Adve2.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="993" data-file-width="666" height="522" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Alsace_Adve2.PNG/350px-Alsace_Adve2.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Alsace_Adve2.PNG/525px-Alsace_Adve2.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/Alsace_Adve2.PNG 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alsace_Adve2.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Administrative map of Alsace showing départements, arrondissements and communes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alsace_Regional_Council" title="Alsace Regional Council">Alsace region</a> is divided into 2 <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_France" title="Departments of France">departments</a>, 13 <a href="/wiki/Arrondissements_of_France" title="Arrondissements of France">departmental arrondissements</a>, 75 <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_France" title="Cantons of France">cantons</a> (not shown here), and 904 <a href="/wiki/Communes_of_France" title="Communes of France">communes</a>:</p>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Bas-Rhin" title="Bas-Rhin">Department of Bas-Rhin</a></b><br/>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alsace_Regional_Council" title="Alsace Regional Council">Alsace Regional Council</a></div>
<p>Alsace is one of the most conservative <i>régions</i> of France. It is one of just two <i>régions</i> in <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_France" title="Metropolitan France">metropolitan France</a> where the conservative right won the <a href="/wiki/French_regional_elections,_2004" title="French regional elections, 2004">2004 <i>région</i> elections</a> and thus controls the <a href="/wiki/Alsace_Regional_Council" title="Alsace Regional Council">Alsace Regional Council</a>. Conservative leader Nicolas Sarkozy got his best score in Alsace (over 65%) in the second round of the <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2007" title="French presidential election, 2007">French presidential elections of 2007</a>. The president of the Regional Council is <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Richert" title="Philippe Richert">Philippe Richert</a>, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_a_Popular_Movement" title="Union for a Popular Movement">Union for a Popular Movement</a>, elected in the 2010 regional election. The frequently changing status of the <i>région</i> throughout history has left its mark on modern day politics in terms of a particular interest in <a href="/wiki/National_identity" title="National identity">national identity</a> issues. Alsace is also one of the most pro-EU regions of France. It was one of the few French regions that voted 'yes' to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Constitution" title="European Constitution">European Constitution</a> in 2005.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alsace's population increased to 1,868,183 in 2013. It has regularly increased over time, except in wartime, by both natural growth and <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migration</a>. This growth has even accelerated at the end of the 20th century. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INSEE" title="INSEE">INSEE</a> estimates that its population will grow 12.9% to 19.5% between 1999 and 2030.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
