<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1927" data-file-width="1927" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG/220px-St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG/330px-St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG/440px-St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St_Stephen%27s_Church_Mulhouse_FRA_001.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Temple_Saint-%C3%89tienne" title="Temple Saint-Étienne">Temple Saint-Étienne</a></i> (architect : <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Schacre" title="Jean-Baptiste Schacre">Jean-Baptiste Schacre</a>), the main <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> church of <a href="/wiki/Mulhouse" title="Mulhouse">Mulhouse</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of the Alsatian population is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, but, largely because of the region's <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Germany" title="Culture of Germany">German</a> heritage, a significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> community also exists: today, the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Church_of_Augsburg_Confession_of_Alsace_and_Lorraine" title="Protestant Church of Augsburg Confession of Alsace and Lorraine">EPCAAL</a> (a Lutheran church) is France's second largest Protestant church, also forming an administrative union (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Union_of_the_Protestant_Churches_of_Alsace_and_Lorraine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Union of the Protestant Churches of Alsace and Lorraine (page does not exist)">UEPAL</a>) with the much smaller Calvinist <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformed_Church_of_Alsace_and_Lorraine" title="Protestant Reformed Church of Alsace and Lorraine">EPRAL</a>. Unlike the rest of France, the <a href="/wiki/Local_law_in_Alsace-Moselle" title="Local law in Alsace-Moselle">Local law in Alsace-Moselle</a> still provides for to the <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_articles" title="Organic articles">organic articles</a>, which provides public subsidies to the Roman Catholic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinist" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> churches, as well as to Jewish synagogues; religion classes in one of these faiths is compulsory in public schools. This divergence in policy from the French majority is due to the region having been part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Germany" title="Imperial Germany">Imperial Germany</a> when the <a href="/wiki/1905_French_law_on_the_Separation_of_the_Churches_and_the_State" title="1905 French law on the Separation of the Churches and the State">1905 law separating the French church and state was instituted</a> (for a more comprehensive history, see: <a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a>). Controversy erupts periodically on the appropriateness of this legal disposition, as well as on the exclusion of other religions from this arrangement.</p>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, promoted by local reformer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bucer" title="Martin Bucer">Martin Bucer</a>, the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Cuius_regio,_eius_religio" title="Cuius regio, eius religio">cuius regio, eius religio</a></i> led to a certain amount of religious diversity in the highlands of northern Alsace. Landowners, who as "local lords" had the right to decide which religion was allowed on their land, were eager to entice populations from the more attractive lowlands to settle and develop their property. Many accepted without discrimination Catholics, Lutherans, Calvinists, Jews and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptists</a>. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/multiconfessional" title="wikt:multiconfessional">Multiconfessional</a> villages appeared, particularly in the region of <a href="/wiki/Alsace_bossue" title="Alsace bossue">Alsace bossue</a>. Alsace became one of the French regions boasting a thriving Jewish community, and the only region with a noticeable Anabaptist population. The schism of the <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">Amish</a> under the lead of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_Amman" title="Jacob Amman">Jacob Amman</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">Mennonites</a> occurred in 1693 in <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Marie-aux-Mines" title="Sainte-Marie-aux-Mines">Sainte-Marie-aux-Mines</a>. The strongly Catholic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> tried in vain to drive them from Alsace. When <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> imposed military conscription without religious exception, most emigrated to the American continent.</p>
<p>In 1707, the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/simultaneum" title="wikt:simultaneum">simultaneum</a> forced many Reformed and Lutheran church buildings to also allow Catholic services. About 50 such "simultaneous churches" still exist in modern Alsace, though with the Catholic church's general lack of priests they tend to hold Catholic services only occasionally.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Alsace historically was part of the Holy Roman Empire and the German realm of culture. Since the 17th century, the region has passed between German and French control numerous times, resulting in a cultural blend. Germanic traits remain in the more traditional, rural parts of the culture, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cuisine" title="Cuisine">cuisine</a> and architecture, whereas modern institutions are totally dominated by French culture.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolism">Symbolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Symbolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="600" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg/220px-Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg/330px-Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg/440px-Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blason_r%C3%A9gion_fr_Alsace.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coats_of_arms" title="Coats of arms">Coats of arms</a> of Alsace.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Strasbourg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="823" data-file-width="745" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg/220px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg/330px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg/440px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Strasbourg.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coats_of_arms" title="Coats of arms">Coats of arms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>'s arms are the colors of the shield of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Strasbourg" title="Bishop of Strasbourg">Bishop of Strasbourg</a> (a band of red on a white field, also considered an inversion of the arms of the diocese) at the end of a revolt of the burghers during the Middle Ages who took their independence from the teachings of the Bishop. It retains its power over the surrounding area.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flags">Flags</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Flags">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Alsace_(historical).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="825" data-file-width="1200" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_Alsace_%28historical%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Alsace_%28historical%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_Alsace_%28historical%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Alsace_%28historical%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_Alsace_%28historical%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Alsace_%28historical%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Alsace_(historical).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rot-un-Wiss, the historical flag</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Alsace_(old).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="261" data-file-width="390" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Flag_of_Alsace_%28old%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Alsace_%28old%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Flag_of_Alsace_%28old%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Alsace_%28old%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Flag_of_Alsace_%28old%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Alsace_%28old%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Alsace_(old).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The region's flag from 1949 to 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is controversy around the recognition of the Alsatian flag. The authentic historical flag is the <i>Rot-un-Wiss</i> ; Red and White are commonly found on the coat of arms of Alsatian cities (Strasbourg, Mulhouse, Sélestat...)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and of many Swiss cites, especially in <a href="/wiki/Basel-Landschaft" title="Basel-Landschaft">Basel's region</a>. The German region <a href="/wiki/Hesse" title="Hesse">Hesse</a> uses a flag similar to the Rot-un-Wiss. As it underlines the Germanic roots of the region, it was <i>replaced</i> in 1949 by a new "Union jack-like" flag representing the union of the two départements. It has, however, no real historical relevance. It has been since replaced again by a slightly different one, also representing the two départements. With the purpose of "Francizing" the region, the Rot-un-Wiss has not been recognized by Paris. Some overzealous statesmen have called it a Nazi invention - while its origins date back to the XIth century and the Red and White banner<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard,_Duke_of_Lorraine" title="Gérard, Duke of Lorraine">Gérard de Lorraine</a> (aka. d'Alsace). The Rot-un-Wiss flag is still known as the real historical emblem of the region by most of the population and the départements' parliaments and has been widely used during protests against the creation of a new "super-region" gathering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Champagne-Ardennes" title="Champagne-Ardennes">Champagne-Ardennes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorraine_(region)" title="Lorraine (region)">Lorraine</a> and Alsace, namely on Colmar's statue of liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alsace_Dialects.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1417" data-file-width="753" height="659" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Alsace_Dialects.PNG/350px-Alsace_Dialects.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Alsace_Dialects.PNG/525px-Alsace_Dialects.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Alsace_Dialects.PNG/700px-Alsace_Dialects.PNG 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alsace_Dialects.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spatial distribution of dialects in Alsace prior to the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Standard_French" title="Standard French">standard French</a> in the 20th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although German dialects were spoken in Alsace for most of its history, the dominant language in Alsace today is French.</p>
<p>The traditional language of the <i>région</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alsatian_language" title="Alsatian language">Alsatian</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic</a> dialect of <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">Upper German</a> spoken on both sides of the Rhine and closely related to <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">Swiss German</a>. Some <a href="/wiki/Frankish_language" title="Frankish language">Frankish</a> dialects of <a href="/wiki/West_Central_German" title="West Central German">West Central German</a> are also spoken in "Alsace Bossue" and in the extreme north of Alsace. Neither Alsatian nor the Frankish dialects have any form of official status, <a href="/wiki/Language_policy_in_France" title="Language policy in France">as is customary</a> for <a href="/wiki/Regional_language" title="Regional language">regional languages</a> in France, although both are now recognized as <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_France" title="Languages of France">languages of France</a> and can be chosen as subjects in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9es" title="Lycées">lycées</a>.</p>
<p>Although Alsace has been part of France multiple times in the past, the region had no direct connection with the French state for several centuries. From the end of the Roman Empire (5th century) to the French annexation (17th century), Alsace was politically part of the Germanic world.</p>
<p>The towns of Alsace were the first to adopt German language as their official language, instead of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Lutheran Reform</a>. It was in Strasbourg that German was first used for the liturgy. It was also in Strasbourg that the first German Bible was published in 1466.</p>
<p>From the annexation of Alsace by France in the 17th century and the language policy of the French Revolution up to 1870, knowledge of French in Alsace increased considerably. With the education reforms of the 19th century, the middle classes began to speak and write French well. The French language never really managed, however, to win over the masses, the vast majority of whom continued to speak their German dialects and write in German (which we would now call "standard German").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="reliable source needed for the whole sentence (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Between 1870 and 1918, Alsace was annexed by the German Empire in the form of an imperial province or Reichsland, and the mandatory official language, especially in schools, became High German. French lost ground to such an extent that it has been estimated that only 2% of the population spoke French fluently and only 8% had some knowledge of it (Maugue, 1970).</p>
<p>After 1918, French was the only language used in schools, and particularly primary schools. After much argument and discussion and after many temporary measures, a memorandum was issued by Vice-Chancellor Pfister in 1927 and governed education in primary schools until 1939.</p>
<p>During a reannexation by Germany (1940–1945), High German was reinstated as the language of education. The population was forced to speak German and 'French' family names were Germanized. Following the Second World War, the 1927 regulation was not reinstated and the teaching of German in primary schools was suspended by a provisional rectorial decree, which was supposed to enable French to regain lost ground. The teaching of German became a major issue, however, as early as 1946. Following World War II, the French government pursued, in line with its traditional <a href="/wiki/Language_policy_in_France" title="Language policy in France">language policy</a>, a campaign to suppress the use of German as part of a wider <a href="/wiki/Francization" title="Francization">Francization</a> campaign.</p>
<p>In 1951, Article 10 of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Deixonne_Law&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Deixonne Law (page does not exist)">Deixonne Law</a> (<i>Loi Deixonne</i>) on the teaching of local languages and dialects made provision for <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a> and old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proven%C3%A7al_(dialect)" title="Provençal (dialect)">Provençal</a>, but not for <a href="/wiki/Corsican_language" title="Corsican language">Corsican</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> (<a href="/wiki/West_Flemish" title="West Flemish">West Flemish</a>) or Alsatian in Alsace and <a href="/wiki/Moselle_(department)" title="Moselle (department)">Moselle</a>. However, in a Decree of 18 December 1952, supplemented by an Order of 19 December of the same year, optional teaching of the German language was introduced in elementary schools in Communes where the language of habitual use was the Alsatian dialect.</p>
<p>In 1972, the Inspector General of German, Georges Holderith, obtained authorization to reintroduce German into 33 intermediate classes on an experimental basis. This teaching of German, referred to as the Holderith Reform, was later extended to all pupils in the last two years of elementary school. This reform is still largely the basis of German teaching (but not Alsatian) in elementary schools today.</p>
<p>It was not until 9 June 1982, with the <i>Circulaire sur la langue et la culture régionales en Alsace</i> (Memorandum on regional language and culture in Alsace) issued by the Vice-Chancellor of the Académie Pierre Deyon, that the teaching of German in primary schools in Alsace really began to be given more official status. The Ministerial Memorandum of 21 June 1982, known as the Circulaire Savary, introduced financial support, over three years, for the teaching of regional languages in schools and universities. This memorandum was, however, implemented in a fairly lax manner.</p>
<p>Both Alsatian and Standard German were for a time banned from public life (including street and city names, official administration, and educational system). Though the ban has long been lifted and street signs today are often bilingual, Alsace-Lorraine is today very French in language and culture. Few young people speak Alsatian today, although there do still exist one or two enclaves in the <a href="/wiki/Sundgau" title="Sundgau">Sundgau</a> region where some older inhabitants cannot speak French, and where Alsatian is still used as the mother tongue. A related <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic German</a> survives on the opposite bank of the Rhine, in <a href="/wiki/Baden" title="Baden">Baden</a>, and especially in Switzerland. However, while French is the major language of the region, the Alsatian dialect of French is heavily influenced by German and other languages such a Yiddish in phonology and vocabulary.</p>
<p>This situation has spurred a movement to preserve the Alsatian language, which is perceived as endangered, a situation paralleled in other <i>régions</i> of France, such as <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> or <a href="/wiki/Occitania" title="Occitania">Occitania</a>. Alsatian is now taught in French high schools. Increasingly, French is the only language used at home and at work, whereas a growing number of people have a good knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">standard German</a> as a foreign language learned in school.</p>
<p>The constitution of the Fifth Republic states that French alone is the official language of the Republic. However, Alsatian, along with other regional languages, are recognized by the French government in the official list of languages of France. A 1999 INSEE survey counted 548,000 adult speakers of Alsatian in France, making it the second most-spoken regional language in the country (after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occitan" title="Occitan">Occitan</a>). Like all regional languages in France, however, the transmission of Alsatian is on the decline. While 39% of the adult population of Alsace speaks Alsatian, only one in four children speaks it, and only one in ten children uses it regularly.</p>
<p>Although the French government signed the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a> in 1992, it never ratified the treaty and therefore no legal basis exists for any of the regional languages in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> However, visitors to Alsace can see indications of renewed political and cultural interest in the language – in Alsatian signs appearing in car-windows and on hoardings, and in new official bilingual street signs in Strasbourg and Mulhouse.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg/220px-Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg/330px-Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg/440px-Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colmar_-_Alsace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Colmar" title="Colmar">Colmar</a>'s old town</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The traditional habitat of the Alsatian lowland, like in other regions of Germany and Northern Europe, consists of houses constructed with walls in <a href="/wiki/Timber_framing" title="Timber framing">timber framing</a> and cob and roofing in flat tiles. This type of construction is abundant in adjacent parts of Germany and can be seen in other areas of France, but their particular abundance in Alsace is owed to several reasons:</p>
<ol>
<li>The proximity to the <a href="/wiki/Vosges" title="Vosges">Vosges</a> where the wood can be found.</li>
<li>During periods of war and bubonic plague, villages were often burned down, so to prevent the collapse of the upper floors, ground floors were built of stone and upper floors built in half-timberings to prevent the spread of fire.</li>
<li>During most of the part of its history, a great part of Alsace was flooded by the Rhine every year. Half-timbered houses were easy to knock down and to move around during those times (a day was necessary to move it and a day to rebuild it in another place).</li>
</ol>
<p>However, half-timbering was found to increase the risk of fire, which is why from the 19th century, it began to be rendered. In recent times, villagers started to paint the rendering white in accordance with Beaux-Arts movements. To discourage this, the region's authorities gave financial grants to the inhabitants to paint the rendering in various colours, in order to return to the original style and many inhabitants accepted (more for financial reasons than by firm belief).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1664" data-file-width="2496" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg/220px-Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg/330px-Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg/440px-Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e_alsacienne_514471722.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Flammekueche</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alsatian_cuisine" title="Alsatian cuisine">Alsatian cuisine</a>, somewhat based on Germanic culinary traditions, is marked by the use of pork in various forms. It is perhaps mostly known for the region's wines and beers. Traditional dishes include <i><a href="/wiki/Baeckeoffe" title="Baeckeoffe">baeckeoffe</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tarte_flamb%C3%A9e" title="Tarte flambée">flammekueche</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Choucroute_garnie" title="Choucroute garnie">choucroute</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fleischnacka" title="Fleischnacka">fleischnacka</a></i>. Southern Alsace, also called the <a href="/wiki/Sundgau" title="Sundgau">Sundgau</a>, is characterized by <i><a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">carpe frite</a></i> (that also exists in <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a> tradition).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The festivities of the year's end involve the production of a great variety of biscuits and small cakes called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bredela" title="Bredela">bredela</a></i> as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Pain_d%27%C3%A9pices" title="Pain d'épices">pain d'épices</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Gingerbread" title="Gingerbread">gingerbread</a> cakes) which are baked around Christmas time.</p>
<p>The gastronomic symbol of the <i>région</i> is undoubtedly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choucroute" title="Choucroute">Choucroute</a>, a local variety of <a href="/wiki/Sauerkraut" title="Sauerkraut">Sauerkraut</a>. The word Sauerkraut in Alsatian has the form <i>sûrkrût</i>, same as in other southwestern German dialects, and means "sour cabbage" as its <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">Standard German</a> equivalent. This word was included into the French language as <i>choucroute</i>. To make it, the cabbage is finely shredded, layered with salt and <a href="/wiki/Juniper" title="Juniper">juniper</a> and left to ferment in wooden barrels. Sauerkraut can be served with poultry, pork, sausage or even fish. Traditionally it is served with Strasbourg sausage or frankfurters, bacon, smoked pork or smoked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morteau_Sausage" title="Morteau Sausage">Morteau</a> or <a href="/wiki/Montb%C3%A9liard" title="Montbéliard">Montbéliard</a> sausages, or a selection of other pork products. Served alongside are often roasted or steamed potatoes or dumplings.</p>
<p>Alsace is also well known for its <a href="/wiki/Foie_gras" title="Foie gras">foie gras</a> made in the region since the 17th century. Additionally, Alsace is known for its fruit juices and mineral waters.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wines">Wines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Wines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg/220px-Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg/330px-Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg/440px-Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Riesling_grapes_leaves.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Riesling Grapes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alsace is an important <a href="/wiki/List_of_wine-producing_regions" title="List of wine-producing regions">wine-producing <i>région</i></a>. <i>Vins d'Alsace</i> (<a href="/wiki/Alsace_wine" title="Alsace wine">Alsace wines</a>) are mostly white. Alsace produces some of the world's most noted dry <a href="/wiki/Riesling" title="Riesling">rieslings</a> and is the only région in France to produce mostly <a href="/wiki/Varietal" title="Varietal">varietal</a> wines identified by the names of the grapes used (wine from <a href="/wiki/Burgundy_wine" title="Burgundy wine">Burgundy</a> is also mainly varietal, but not normally identified as such), typically from grapes also used in Germany. The most notable example is <a href="/wiki/Gew%C3%BCrztraminer" title="Gewürztraminer">Gewürztraminer</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beers">Beers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Beers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kronenbourg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="515" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Kronenbourg.jpg/220px-Kronenbourg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Kronenbourg.jpg/330px-Kronenbourg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Kronenbourg.jpg/440px-Kronenbourg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kronenbourg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kronenbourg" title="Kronenbourg">Kronenbourg</a> brewery.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alsace is also the main beer-producing region of France, thanks primarily to <a href="/wiki/Brewery" title="Brewery">breweries</a> in and near <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>. These include those of <a href="/wiki/Fischer_Brewery" title="Fischer Brewery">Fischer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karlsbr%C3%A4u" title="Karlsbräu">Karlsbräu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kronenbourg" title="Kronenbourg">Kronenbourg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heineken_International" title="Heineken International">Heineken International</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">Hops</a> are grown in <a href="/wiki/Kochersberg" title="Kochersberg">Kochersberg</a> and in northern Alsace. <a href="/wiki/Schnapps" title="Schnapps">Schnapps</a> is also traditionally made in Alsace, but it is in decline because home <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillers</a> are becoming less common and the consumption of traditional, strong, alcoholic beverages is decreasing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trivia">Trivia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Trivia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg/220px-Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg/330px-Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg/440px-Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cegonha_alsaciana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alsatian stork</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">stork</a> is a main feature of Alsace and was the subject of many <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">legends</a> told to children. The bird practically disappeared around 1970, but re-population efforts are continuing. They are mostly found on roofs of houses, churches and other public buildings in Alsace.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Easter_Bunny" title="Easter Bunny">Easter Bunny</a> was first mentioned in <a href="/wiki/Georg_Franck_von_Franckenau" title="Georg Franck von Franckenau">Georg Franck von Franckenau</a>'s <i>De ovis paschalibus</i> (About Easter Eggs) in 1682 referring to an Alsace tradition of an Easter Hare bringing Easter Eggs.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_the_term_.22Alsatia.22_in_English">Use of the term "Alsatia" in English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title='Edit section: Use of the term "Alsatia" in English'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alsatia" title="Alsatia">Alsatia</a></div>
<p>"Alsatia", the Latin form of Alsace's name, has long ago entered the English language with the specialized meaning of "a lawless place" or "a place under no jurisdiction" - since Alsace was conceived by English people to be such. It was used into the 20th century as a term for a ramshackle marketplace, "protected by ancient custom and the independence of their patrons". As of 2007, the word is still in use among the English and Australian judiciaries with the meaning of a place where the law cannot reach: "In setting up the <a href="/wiki/Serious_Organised_Crime_Agency" title="Serious Organised Crime Agency">Serious Organised Crime Agency</a>, the state has set out to create an Alsatia - a region of executive action free of judicial oversight," <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Justice_Sedley" title="Lord Justice Sedley">Lord Justice Sedley</a> in UMBS v SOCA 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Derived from the above, "<a href="/wiki/Alsatia" title="Alsatia">Alsatia</a>" was historically a <a href="/wiki/Cant_(language)" title="Cant (language)">cant</a> term for the area near <a href="/wiki/Whitefriars,_London" title="Whitefriars, London">Whitefriars, London</a>, which was for a long time a <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary" title="Sanctuary">sanctuary</a>. It is first known in print in the title of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Squire_of_Alsatia" title="The Squire of Alsatia">The Squire of Alsatia</a></i>, a 1688 play written by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Shadwell" title="Thomas Shadwell">Thomas Shadwell</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the <i>Institut National de la Statistique et des Études Économiques</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INSEE" title="INSEE">INSEE</a>), Alsace had a gross domestic product of 44.3 billion euros in 2002. With a GDP per capita of €24,804, it was the second-place <i>région</i> of France, losing only to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France_(region)" title="Île-de-France (region)">Île-de-France</a>. 68% of its jobs are in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Service_Sector" title="Service Sector">services</a>; 25% are in industry, making Alsace one of France's most industrialised <i>régions</i>.</p>
<p>Alsace is a <i>région</i> of varied economic activity, including:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Viticulture" title="Viticulture">viticulture</a> (mostly along the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Route_des_Vins_d%27Alsace" title="Route des Vins d'Alsace">Route des Vins d'Alsace</a></i> between <a href="/wiki/Marlenheim" title="Marlenheim">Marlenheim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thann,_Haut-Rhin" title="Thann, Haut-Rhin">Thann</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hop</a> harvesting and brewing (half of French beer is produced in Alsace, especially in the vicinity of Strasbourg, notably in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kronenbourg" title="Kronenbourg">Strasbourg-Cronenbourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schiltigheim" title="Schiltigheim">Schiltigheim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Obernai" title="Obernai">Obernai</a>)</li>
<li>forestry development</li>
<li>automobile industry (<a href="/wiki/Mulhouse" title="Mulhouse">Mulhouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molsheim" title="Molsheim">Molsheim</a>, home town of <a href="/wiki/Bugatti" title="Bugatti">Bugatti</a> Automobiles)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_science" title="Life science">life sciences</a>, as part of the trinational <a href="/wiki/BioValley_(Europe)" title="BioValley (Europe)">BioValley</a> and</li>
<li>tourism</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> (until the late 20th century) and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> mining</li>
</ul>
<p>Alsace has many international ties and 35% of firms are foreign companies (notably German, Swiss, American, Japanese, and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Having been early and always densely populated, Alsace is famous for its high number of picturesque villages, churches and castles and for the various beauties of its three main towns, in spite of severe destructions suffered throughout five centuries of wars between France and Germany.</p>
<p>Alsace is furthermore famous for its vineyards (especially along the 170 km of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Route_des_Vins_d%27Alsace" title="Route des Vins d'Alsace">Route des Vins d'Alsace</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Marlenheim" title="Marlenheim">Marlenheim</a> to <a href="/wiki/Thann,_Haut-Rhin" title="Thann, Haut-Rhin">Thann</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vosges_mountains" title="Vosges mountains">Vosges mountains</a> with their thick and green forests and picturesque lakes.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg/220px-Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg/330px-Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg/440px-Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haut-koenigsbourg_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_du_Haut-K%C5%93nigsbourg" title="Château du Haut-Kœnigsbourg">Château du Haut-Kœnigsbourg</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maginot_line_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Maginot_line_1.jpg/220px-Maginot_line_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Maginot_line_1.jpg/330px-Maginot_line_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Maginot_line_1.jpg/440px-Maginot_line_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

The main entrance of the <a href="/wiki/Ouvrage_Schoenenbourg" title="Ouvrage Schoenenbourg">Ouvrage Schoenenbourg</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Maginot_Line" title="Maginot Line">Maginot Line</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Old towns of <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colmar" title="Colmar">Colmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9lestat" title="Sélestat">Sélestat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guebwiller" title="Guebwiller">Guebwiller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saverne" title="Saverne">Saverne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obernai" title="Obernai">Obernai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thann,_Haut-Rhin" title="Thann, Haut-Rhin">Thann</a></li>
<li>Smaller cities and villages: <a href="/wiki/Molsheim" title="Molsheim">Molsheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosheim" title="Rosheim">Rosheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riquewihr" title="Riquewihr">Riquewihr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ribeauvill%C3%A9" title="Ribeauvillé">Ribeauvillé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaysersberg" title="Kaysersberg">Kaysersberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wissembourg" title="Wissembourg">Wissembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuwiller-l%C3%A8s-Saverne" title="Neuwiller-lès-Saverne">Neuwiller-lès-Saverne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marmoutier" title="Marmoutier">Marmoutier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rouffach" title="Rouffach">Rouffach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soultz-Haut-Rhin" title="Soultz-Haut-Rhin">Soultz-Haut-Rhin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bergheim,_Haut-Rhin" title="Bergheim, Haut-Rhin">Bergheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunspach" title="Hunspach">Hunspach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seebach,_Bas-Rhin" title="Seebach, Bas-Rhin">Seebach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turckheim" title="Turckheim">Turckheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eguisheim" title="Eguisheim">Eguisheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuf-Brisach" title="Neuf-Brisach">Neuf-Brisach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferrette" title="Ferrette">Ferrette</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niedermorschwihr" title="Niedermorschwihr">Niedermorschwihr</a> and the gardens of the blue house in <a href="/wiki/Uttenhoffen" title="Uttenhoffen">Uttenhoffen</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>Churches (as main sights in otherwise less remarkable places): <a href="/wiki/Thann,_Haut-Rhin" title="Thann, Haut-Rhin">Thann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andlau" title="Andlau">Andlau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Murbach" title="Murbach">Murbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ebersmunster" title="Ebersmunster">Ebersmunster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niederhaslach" title="Niederhaslach">Niederhaslach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigolsheim" title="Sigolsheim">Sigolsheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lautenbach" title="Lautenbach">Lautenbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epfig" title="Epfig">Epfig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altorf" title="Altorf">Altorf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottmarsheim" title="Ottmarsheim">Ottmarsheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domfessel" title="Domfessel">Domfessel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niederhaslach" title="Niederhaslach">Niederhaslach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marmoutier" title="Marmoutier">Marmoutier</a> and the fortified church at <a href="/wiki/Hunawihr" title="Hunawihr">Hunawihr</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_du_Haut-K%C5%93nigsbourg" title="Château du Haut-Kœnigsbourg">Château du Haut-Kœnigsbourg</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_castles_in_France#Alsace" title="List of castles in France">Other castles</a>: Ortenbourg and <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Ramstein_(Bas-Rhin)" title="Château de Ramstein (Bas-Rhin)">Ramstein</a> (above Sélestat), Hohlandsbourg, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_du_Fleckenstein" title="Château du Fleckenstein">Fleckenstein</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haut-Barr" title="Haut-Barr">Haut-Barr</a> (above Saverne), Saint-Ulrich (above Ribeauvillé), Lichtenberg, Wangenbourg, the three Castles of <a href="/wiki/Eguisheim" title="Eguisheim">Eguisheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Pflixbourg" title="Château de Pflixbourg">Pflixbourg</a>, Wasigenstein, <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_d%27Andlau" title="Château d'Andlau">Andlau</a>, Grand Geroldseck, <a href="/wiki/Wasenbourg" title="Wasenbourg">Wasenbourg</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cit%C3%A9_de_l%27Automobile" title="Cité de l'Automobile">Cité de l'Automobile</a> museum in Mulhouse</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cit%C3%A9_du_train" title="Cité du train">Cité du train</a> museum in Mulhouse</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Ungersheim" title="Ungersheim">Ungersheim</a>'s "<i>écomusée</i>" (open-air museum) and "<i><a href="/wiki/Le_Bioscope" title="Le Bioscope">Bioscope</a></i>" (leisure park about the environment, closed since September 2012)</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Odile_of_Alsace" title="Odile of Alsace">Mount Ste Odile</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Route_des_Vins_d%27Alsace" title="Route des Vins d'Alsace">Route des Vins d'Alsace</a> (Alsace Wine Route)</li>
<li>Mémorial d'Alsace-Lorraine in <a href="/wiki/Schirmeck" title="Schirmeck">Schirmeck</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Natzweiler-Struthof" title="Natzweiler-Struthof">Natzweiler-Struthof</a>, the only German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentration_camp" title="Concentration camp">concentration camp</a> on French territory during WWII</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vosges_Mountains" title="Vosges Mountains">Famous mountains</a>: Massif du Donon, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Ballon" title="Grand Ballon">Grand Ballon</a>, Petit Ballon, <a href="/wiki/Ballon_d%27Alsace" title="Ballon d'Alsace">Ballon d'Alsace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hohneck_(Vosges)" title="Hohneck (Vosges)">Hohneck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hartmannswillerkopf" title="Hartmannswillerkopf">Hartmannswillerkopf</a></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Roads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg/220px-Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg/330px-Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg/440px-Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Absolute_ponts_couverts_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponts_Couverts_(Strasbourg)" title="Ponts Couverts (Strasbourg)">Ponts Couverts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most major car journeys are made on the <a href="/wiki/A35_autoroute" title="A35 autoroute">A35</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">autoroute</a>, which links Saint-Louis on the Swiss border to Lauterbourg on the German border.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/A4_autoroute" title="A4 autoroute">A4</a> toll road (towards Paris) begins 20 km northwest of <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a> and the <a href="/wiki/A36_autoroute" title="A36 autoroute">A36</a> toll road towards Lyon, begins 10 km west from <a href="/wiki/Mulhouse" title="Mulhouse">Mulhouse</a>.</p>
<p>Spaghetti-junctions (built in the 1970s and 1980s) are prominent in the comprehensive system of motorways in Alsace, especially in the outlying areas of Strasbourg and Mulhouse. These cause a major buildup of traffic and are the main sources of pollution in the towns, notably in Strasbourg where the motorway traffic of the A35 was 170,000 per day in 2002.</p>
<p>At present, plans are being considered for building a new <a href="/wiki/Dual_carriageway" title="Dual carriageway">dual carriageway</a> west of Strasbourg, which would reduce the buildup of traffic in that area by picking up north and southbound vehicles and getting rid of the buildup outside Strasbourg. The line plans to link up the interchange of <a href="/wiki/H%C5%93rdt" title="Hœrdt">Hœrdt</a> to the north of Strasbourg, with <a href="/wiki/Innenheim" title="Innenheim">Innenheim</a> in the southwest. The opening is envisaged at the end of 2011, with an average usage of 41,000 vehicles a day. Estimates of the French Works Commissioner however, raised some doubts over the interest of such a project, since it would pick up only about 10% of the traffic of the A35 at Strasbourg. Paradoxically, this reversed the situation of the 1950s. At that time, the French trunk road left of the Rhine not been built, so that traffic would cross into Germany to use the Karlsruhe-Basel Autobahn.</p>
<p>To add to the buildup of traffic, the neighbouring German state of <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a> has imposed a tax on heavy-goods vehicles using their <a href="/wiki/Autobahn" title="Autobahn">Autobahnen</a>. Thus, a proportion of the HGVs travelling from north Germany to Switzerland or southern Alsace bypasses the <a href="/wiki/Bundesautobahn_5" title="Bundesautobahn 5">A5</a> on the Alsace-Baden-Württemberg border and uses the untolled, French <a href="/wiki/A35_autoroute" title="A35 autoroute">A35</a> instead.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trains">Trains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Trains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="659" data-file-width="808" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg/220px-Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg/330px-Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg/440px-Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Place_de_l_Homme_de_Fer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Place de l'Homme de Fer Tram Station</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/TER_Alsace" title="TER Alsace">TER Alsace</a> is the rail network serving Alsace. Its network is articulated around the city of Strasbourg. It is one of the most developed rail networks in France, financially sustained partly by the French railroad <a href="/wiki/SNCF" title="SNCF">SNCF</a>, and partly by the <i>région</i> Alsace.</p>
<p>Because the Vosges are surmountable only by the <a href="/wiki/Col_de_Saverne" title="Col de Saverne">Col de Saverne</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Belfort" title="Belfort">Belfort</a> Gap, it has been suggested that Alsace needs to open up and get closer to France in terms of its rail links. Developments already under way or planned include:</p>
<ul>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/LGV_Est" title="LGV Est">TGV Est</a> (Paris – Strasbourg) had its first phase brought into service in June 2007, bringing down the Strasbourg-Paris trip from 4 hours to 2 hours 20 minutes. Work on its second phase, which will further bring down this time to 1hour 50 minutes, is due to be completed in 2016.</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/LGV_Rhin-Rh%C3%B4ne" title="LGV Rhin-Rhône">TGV Rhin-Rhône</a> between <a href="/wiki/Dijon" title="Dijon">Dijon</a> and Mulhouse (opened in 2011)</li>
<li>a tram-train system in Mulhouse (2011)</li>
<li>an interconnection with the German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/InterCityExpress" title="InterCityExpress">InterCityExpress</a>, as far as <a href="/wiki/Kehl" title="Kehl">Kehl</a> (expected 2016)</li>
</ul>
<p>However, the abandoned Maurice-Lemaire tunnel towards <a href="/wiki/Saint-Di%C3%A9-des-Vosges" title="Saint-Dié-des-Vosges">Saint-Dié-des-Vosges</a> was rebuilt as a toll road.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waterways">Waterways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Waterways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Port traffic of Alsace exceeds 15 million tonnes, of which about three-quarters is centred on Strasbourg, which is the second busiest French fluvial harbour. The enlargement plan of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne%E2%80%93Rhine_Canal" title="Rhône–Rhine Canal">Rhône–Rhine Canal</a>, intended to link up the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and Central Europe (Rhine, <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>) was abandoned in 1998 for reasons of expense and land erosion, notably in the Doubs valley.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_traffic">Air traffic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Air traffic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are two <a href="/wiki/International_airport" title="International airport">international airports</a> in Alsace:</p>
<ul>
<li>the international airport of Strasbourg in <a href="/wiki/Entzheim" title="Entzheim">Entzheim</a></li>
<li>the international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EuroAirport_Basel-Mulhouse-Freiburg" title="EuroAirport Basel-Mulhouse-Freiburg">EuroAirport Basel-Mulhouse-Freiburg</a>, which is the seventh largest French airport in terms of traffic</li>
</ul>
<p>Strasbourg is also two hours away by road from one of the largest European airports, Frankfurt Main, and 2 hour 30 minutes from <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle_Airport" title="Charles de Gaulle Airport">Charles de Gaulle Airport</a> through the direct <a href="/wiki/TGV" title="TGV">TGV</a> service, stopping in Terminal 2.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cycling_network">Cycling network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Cycling network">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Crossed by three <a href="/wiki/EuroVelo" title="EuroVelo">EuroVelo</a> routes</p>
<ul>
<li>the EuroVelo 5 (<a href="/wiki/Via_Francigena" title="Via Francigena">Via Francigena</a> from London to Rome/<a href="/wiki/Brindisi" title="Brindisi">Brindisi</a>),</li>
<li>the EuroVelo 6 (Véloroute des fleuves from <a href="/wiki/Nantes" title="Nantes">Nantes</a> to <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a> (H)) and</li>
<li>the EuroVelo 15 (Véloroute Rhin / Rhine cycle route from <a href="/wiki/Andermatt" title="Andermatt">Andermatt</a> (CH) to <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a> (NL)).</li>
</ul>
<p>Alsace is the most well equipped region of France, with 2000 kilometres of cycle routes. The network is of a very good standard and well signposted. All the towpaths of the canals in Alsace (<a href="/wiki/Canal_des_houill%C3%A8res_de_la_Sarre" title="Canal des houillères de la Sarre">canal des houillères de la Sarre</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canal_de_la_Marne_au_Rhin" title="Canal de la Marne au Rhin">canal de la Marne au Rhin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canal_de_la_Bruche" title="Canal de la Bruche">canal de la Bruche</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canal_du_Rh%C3%B4ne_au_Rhin" title="Canal du Rhône au Rhin">canal du Rhône au Rhin</a>) are tarred.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sister_provinces">Sister provinces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alsace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Sister provinces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is an <i>accord de coopération internationale</i> between Alsace and the following regions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea"><img alt="South Korea" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/23px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/35px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/45px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyeongsangbuk-do" title="Gyeongsangbuk-do">Gyeongsangbuk-do</a>, South Korea</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland"><img alt="Poland" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1280" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/23px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/35px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/46px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Lower_Silesian_Voivodeship" title="Lower Silesian Voivodeship">Lower Silesia</a>, Poland</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria"><img alt="Austria" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/23px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/35px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_Austria.svg/45px-Flag_of_Austria.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Upper_Austria" title="Upper Austria">Upper Austria</a>, Austria</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada"><img alt="Canada" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="1000" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/23px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/35px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/46px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, Canada</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/China" title="China"><img alt="China" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, China</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia"><img alt="Russia" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Oblast" title="Moscow Oblast">Moscow</a>, Russia</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania"><img alt="Romania" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Flag_of_Romania.svg/23px-Flag_of_Romania.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Flag_of_Romania.svg/35px-Flag_of_Romania.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Flag_of_Romania.svg/45px-Flag_of_Romania.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Vest_(development_region)" title="Vest (development region)">Vest</a>, Romania</li>
</ul>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Alsace#History" title="Alsace">History</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">

<li><a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a> (circa 213–496)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alamannia" title="Alamannia">Alamannia</a> (3rd-century–911)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Alsace" title="Duchy of Alsace">Duchy of Alsace</a> (circa 630–699)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishopric_of_Strasbourg" title="Prince-Bishopric of Strasbourg">Prince-Bishopric of Strasbourg</a> (982–1803)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ferrette#County_of_Ferrette" title="Ferrette">County of Ferrette</a> (11th-century–14th-century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salm_(state)" title="Salm (state)">Salm</a> (1165−1793)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Further_Austria" title="Further Austria">Further Austria</a> (13th-century–1648)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9capole" title="Décapole">Décapole</a> (1354–1679)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hanau-Lichtenberg" title="Hanau-Lichtenberg">County of Hanau-Lichtenberg</a> (1456–1736)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Imperial Territory of Alsace-Lorraine</a> (1871–1918)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gau_Baden-Elsa%C3%9F" title="Gau Baden-Elsaß">Gau Baden-Elsaß</a> (1940–1945)</li>
<li><strong class="selflink">Alsace</strong> (1945–2016)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grand_Est" title="Grand Est">Grand Est</a> (2016–)</li>
</ul>
